0060873027|4.0|As a fan of Michael Crichton I was excited to hear that his final, incomplete project would be finished and published though I had my worries as to who was up to the task.  When I discovered that Richard Preston had been chosen to take the manuscript to the finish line I breathed a sigh of relief.  I had read The Hot Zone several years ago and really enjoyed that thrilling tale of terror in which Preston handled the science, medical and biological, aspects of the story well and I could see him doing the same in a Crichtonian way.  Micro has the typical Crichton setup, scientists of various disciplines are gathered together by a person who is using a new technology that has both great promise and great danger.  The main action of the book occurs on the island of Oah'u Hawai'i, where I recently was conducting research and completing graduate school so I immediately felt some familiarity with the characters.  The authors include many little details of life in Hawaii, simple customs, places, foods and such, that added a nice realistic backdrop to the story.  This realism is especially extended into the insect/arachnid scale world of Hawaii in which the micro-miniaturized heros find themselves.  I felt the beginning of the book was a little slow but picked up my pace and interest as I progressed.  The appearances of the creatures in this micro world are accurate, from the wasps and terrible Hawaiian ant problem to the dreaded vemonous centipede.  The contrast between the extremely benign macroscopic Hawaii and the savage micro world is very stark.<br /><br />I often felt like I was reading Jurassic Park again, but in reverse.  Instead of giant lizards eating people the people are now micro being attacked by bugs.  However, I give credit to the authors that some unexpected events take place which I feel were rather gutsy from a storytelling standpoint which differentiates it a bit from Jurassic Park.  There is a good deal of action and details of the micro world's biologythat keep things interesting.  Micro is not great literature or a deep thinker, but the adventure is fun and Preston has done a good job of completing Crichton's final novel for the fans.  Read Micro for fun, not solemn seriousness.

0061692042|3.0|In the novel Canada, the youth Dell grasps at his \\"last best chance\\" but wonders ever after if he's dodged some punishment due.<br /><br />Upon releasing his 10th book of fiction, the American Richard Ford still cuts a fine figure.  By which I refer to the 60,000 sales claimed for his previous novel The Lay of the Land.  Even in the American market, many literary novelists would settle for a fraction of that.<br /><br />As women dominate fiction buying and reading, it must be that women are reading Ford.  And why not?  Sure, he impressed early on with the brisk drugs-and-crime novel The Ultimate Good Luck.  But later came Women With Men.  And his Pulitzer Prize winner Independence Day is a more suburban affair, with shades of John Cheever.<br /><br />Canada I found to have a sharper edge than Independence Day or The Lay of the Land. To some degree, it is a darker and longer reprise of Ford's Wildlife novel.  Each employs the setting of Great Falls, Montana.  In each, a thoughtful teenage observer is disarrayed by a sudden and major family disturbance.<br /><br />The author has retained his urge to tip things off their axis to see what will happen.  No chance for spoiler alerts, not with these first two sentences: \\"First I'll tell you about the robbery our parents committed. Then about the murders, which happened later.\\"<br /><br />That's the first of several signals, which serve to silhouette certain misfortunes lurking some pages ahead for the principal players.  This ploy has the reader juggling the narrative tensions of both (to borrow Donald Rumsfeld's slippery remark) known unknowns and unknown unknowns.<br /><br />More so than himself as a younger writer, Ford will get to wherever he's going in his own good time.  Along the way, we should get to know the people, how they look, what they wear, their scents and their personal effects.  The weather and the seasons are to be experienced, the townscapes and the landscapes.<br /><br />For example, Dell as the narrator is soon to concede that the heist was something his father Bev had always itched for.  That his mother Neeva had always owned the weakness that would let her go along with it.<br /><br />But \\"none of this is worth hearing\\" without first displaying the family's normal side.  They are to be seen in their fine summer moments, when \\"the '55 Chevrolet is at the curb in the stalky elm shade under the soft Montana sky\\".<br /><br />Bev pictures himself as a modern man.  He's smart enough to use fancy words like \\"hierarchy\\" and \\"hauteur\\".  He's reckless enough to imagine Dell, not Neeva, as his first-choice accomplice.  This Dell discovers only years afterwards.  But the reader finds out soon after the heist.  Turns out it's another signal, one that is strongly related to events and themes that will only emerge late in the piece.<br /><br />After the robbery, Dell endures a difficult Canadian sojourn.  The key settings there, a real and a derelict township, are rendered just as faithfully as Great Falls.  By way of consolation, the deserted buildings give Dell some sense of starting over.<br /><br />In this part, biblically, men come to trouble as the sparks fly upward.  \\"To say something's founded on a lie,\\" one such man remarks, \\"isn't really alleging that much\\".  Dell's forced to realise that we're all born to deception.<br /><br />\\"Impersonation and deception,\\" so the mature Dell instructs, \\"are the great themes of American literature.\\"  Ford can almost be imagined giving similar counsel at Ole Miss (the University of Mississippi) where recently he took up a chair in English and creative writing.<br /><br />Dell's lesson in literature, nevertheless, looks quite different to his lessons of life.  Life is passed along to us empty, is his conclusion, with hidden meaning all but absent.  He believes in \\"what you see being most of what there is\\".  Which is not so far away from the aphoristic acronym of computing, WYSIWYG, what you see is what you get.<br /><br />(CanberraTimes, June 2012)

1594744769|4.0|Jacob loves his grandfather and is devastated when he dies.  He was brought up on his grandfather's strange stories of the time when he was a resident in Miss Peregrine's home for peculiar children off the Welsh coast.  Jacob loved the odd photographs his grandfather used to show him even though he was convinced they had been tampered with and that the stories that went with them were just fairy tales.  When he sees a monster at the scene of his grandfather's death and keeps having nightmares he is taken to see a psychiatrist.<br /><br /> Jacob wants to make a trip from his home in America to the Welsh island to see if he can find Miss Peregrine as she apparently wrote to his grandfather fifteen years ago.  Jacob manages to convince his parents that the trip would be good for him and his father decides to combine it with some bird-watching. When they arrive on the island Jacob starts to wonder where the truth ends and fantasy begins.<br /><br />This is a strange book.  Is it fact?  Is it fiction?  The photographs - created years before computers - are genuine and very strange.  There is nothing horrific about them - no one is dead, there is no violence but they still leave some very disturbing images in the mind of the reader.<br /><br />Even without the photographs this would be a strange story - a blend of the present day and the recent past.  With the photographs - the girl levitating, the man holding a boulder over his head in one hand, the head with a face painted on the back among others - that extra dimension is added to the book which lifts it out of the ordinary.  Is it a book for adults or children?  Some children will love it - others may be disturbed and have nightmares as Jacob did.<br /><br />The book is well written and it is an interesting story prompting thoughts in the reader about the fine line between reality and fantasy.  I found it disturbing and fascinating reading.

B004RE29PO|4.0|Suspenseful and terrifying like the original, but in completely different ways. Where Ridley Scott's first film was loaded up with atmosphere, exploration and an overwhelming sense of terrified wonder at the habits of an unfamiliar life form, James Cameron's sequel is faced with the task of expanding upon a theme that's already been fairly thoroughly prodded. Dropping the intellectual angles and dramatic pauses in favor of unbridled, run-and-gun chaos, it still manages to feel akin to its predecessor via more than a monster suit and lead actress. While Aliens doesn't take as much time to appreciate the sights, they're still around, and they remain excellent examples of on-screen world building. The power of the aliens themselves seems very diminished - they're little more than waves of cannon fodder by the second act - but Cameron compensates with a bold determination to examine them as a versatile breed of fauna. Although its dalliances into action tripes can be distracting, and a few lines of dialog are eyeball-spinningly cheeseball, the film is still a success. How much of that is due to its young director's touch, rather than the power of the galaxy that had been established in the original, is open for debate.

0307716546|5.0|I love a good murder mystery and I certainly got that and a whole lot more in the novel Afterwards by Rosamund Lupton. I had read Lupton's first novel, Sister, and thoroughly enjoyed it for the unique plotting, page turning prose and a terrific gut punching twist at the end. But I digress.<br /><br />Afterwards is Lupton's second book and I found it just as uniquely crafted as Sister but in a whole different way. Told by the disembodied spirits of a mother and daughter, victims of an arsonist, this book could go campy right away, but in the capable hands of Lupton the reader somehow manages to accept the storytellers and just get on with trying to solve the mystery.<br /><br />Grace and Jenny, the mom and teenage daughter, are both in comas due to the traumatic injuries they've suffered in a fire at the school where Jenny is working and where Grace's other child Adam is still a student. The reader must suspend belief when Grace and Jenny leave their bodies and find each other in the hallways of the hospital. It is through Grace's watchful spirit that the story unfolds and we the readers get to observe as the identity of the arsonist and the motive are revealed.<br /><br />I can see how some readers may feel the novel is gimmicky, but I think Lupton is such a readable author with such a fluid narrative style that I would emphatically disagree. There are so many books that I pick up that feel like drudgery to complete, but not so with Rosamund Lupton. Even though she only has two books under her belt, she is one of my favorite authors.

B006ZCWTXS|4.0|British X-Factor finalist Rebecca Ferguson's debut effort [[ASIN:B006ZCWTXS Heaven (US Bonus Track Version)]] materializes in the United States in 2012; it first appeared in the U.K. In 2011.  The album is a neo-soul affair, with pop sensibility, that well suits the gritty, soulful Ferguson.  Ferguson possesses a strong, captivating presence throughout the eleven tracks of Heaven, always remaining in `good' voice, even when some material is less alluring than other material.  Overall, Heaven proves to be an interesting and compelling debut.<br /><br />\\"Nothing's Real But Love,\\" produced/co-written by Eg White, opens Heaven soulfully with a roots-driven, neo-soul sound, owed to the use of organ, drums, and acoustic guitar.  Ferguson possesses an incredibly gritty voice with more than enough charisma to sell the opener.  The songwriting is notable, particularly on the refrain in which Ferguson sings: \\"And nothing's real but love/nothing's real but love/no money, no house, no car, can be love...\\" \\"Glitter and Gold\\" again highlights the smoky nature of Ferguson's voice.  The cut sounds indigenous to the 1970s, but not so much so that it is an anachronism in 2012. Supporting background vocals accentuate Ferguson's stunning lead, further making the chanteuse shine.<br /><br />\\"Shoulder to Shoulder\\" is characterized by big thudding drums demarcating beats one and three (the strong beats).  Ferguson's voice continues to draw the listener in, particularly as she performs the refrain: \\"And we fight and we cry/and we tell the same lies about love/and we cling to each other's shoulder to shoulder against the world...\\" \\"Fairytale (Let Me Live My Life This Way)\\" continues the vintage soul scheme through-and-through, yielding some of the strongest production of the alum.  Ferguson is on autopilot here, delivering perhaps her most commanding vocals of the entirety of the effort.  Production-wise, the big drums and groove are a selling point.<br /><br />\\"Mr. Bright Eyes,\\" produced by Xenomania is as vintage as things go. Characterized by the rhythmic guitar chords on beats two and four and loud live drums, \\"Mr. Bright Eyes\\" would have been a perfect choice for the late, great Amy Winehouse or Etta James.  The loudness of the vocals recalls 1950s R&B and rock-n-roll where `shouty' vocals where characteristic of the style.  \\"Fighting Suspicions,\\" another Eg White co-write/production,  proves to be a bit `all-over-the-place.' The cut takes an unsurprising change of pace early on, around the :50 mark.  There are some nice musical moments, but the cut does not feel as well conceived as previous ones.  Vocally, Ferguson concedes no momentum, continuing to sing her heart out.<br /><br />\\"Teach Me How to Be Loved\\" is a better cut, continuing to allow Ferguson's voice to shimmer in grace.  The sole quibble of \\"Teach Me How To Be Loved\\" might be that the cut could use more `direction' and accompaniment to make it more interesting and suspending to the listener.  \\"Run Free\\" marks an `upswing' for Heaven, delivering a sound cut with driven groove, clear vocals, and a well penned refrain.  The production here is less steeped in `soulful' vocal production, which gives this cut a slightly `cleaner' sound.<br /><br />\\"Diamond to Stone\\" continues the overall allure of Heaven yielding a soulful, relatively catchy cut.  Penultimate cut \\"Too Good To Lose\\" is a winner, providing Ferguson with a more modern flare.  There is still a soul about the cut, but the more contemporary sound appeals to a younger demographic.  Bonus cut \\"Backtrack\\" is solid, with a driving, soulful production.<br /><br />Overall, [[ASIN:B006ZCWTXS Heaven (US Bonus Track Version)]] is a solid debut from the X-Factor contestant.  There are no overt misses and Ferguson's vocals always remain solid and soulful.  Highly recommended debut effort overall.y Xenomania is as vintage as things go. B000002MU2|5.0|I was glancing through reviews of this album and ALL of the poor ones said things like \\"I've listened to them since the beginning and this sucks!\\" or \\"It's not the R.E.M that I grew up with...\\" or \\"It's their lame attempt at cashing in on grunge!\\" or most especially \\"They should've broken up after AUTOMATIC FOR THE PEOPLE!\\". While I understand that music, like all art, is subjective, I can't fathom the \\"fans\\" that don't see anything of value on an album that has some of their best songs ever. After the one-two mega-success punch of OUT OF TIME and AUTOMATIC, R.E.M knew that they were pretty much on top of the world. They were all over the radio; they were all over MTV (For you young people, MTV actually stands for Music Television, and they used to play lots and lots of music!); and they were about to tour all over the world. To be totally honest, after their massive success, if they decided to tour based on the work of OUT OF TIME and/or AFTP and continue with smaller venues, it would have been a colossal mistake. They were about to become arena rockers. They needed a rock and roll album that fit their aesthetic.<br /><br />That idea of a R.E.M Arena Rocker Album is what gave birth to MONSTER. Out of this album, not only was there a distinctly different sound in contrast to the flowing melodies of OUT OF TIME and sorrowful tone of AFTP, there was also a distinctly different look to the band. Michael came out, head shaved to the scalp with a greater spring in his step and a Elvis-pelvis, but Mike also came out in amazingly hilarious glam-rock leisure suits (all of them the same, except the color). These were no longer the shy post-collegians with their art-rock style; this was an R.E.M we had never seen before and certainly hadn't heard before.<br /><br />From the most anthemic opening to an R.E.M album that has ever existed with \\"What's The Frequency, Kenneth?\\" to the Sonic Youth/Iggy and The Stooges-influenced fuzz-ridden \\"Crush With Eyeliner\\" to the loin-straining \\"I Don't Sleep, I Dream\\" to the hard pounding pulse of \\"Star 69\\" to the brightly melodic \\"Strange Currencies\\" to the tragic and erotic imagery of \\"Bang and Blame\\" to the drunken torch-song piano lyricism of \\"Tongue\\" to the crazy angry distortion of \\"I Took Your Name\\" to the epic fuzziness of \\"Circus Envy\\" to the rhythmic, sexually-charged finale of \\"You\\", it's a pretty amazing album from start to finish.<br /><br />You might have noticed there were two songs I didn't include: \\"King of Comedy\\" and \\"Let Me In\\". \\"King of Comedy\\" is an interesting enough indictment of celebrity-personal-life-as-entertainment, but musically, it's pretty weak. That's okay, though. Even on the very best albums, there's usually one weak song. \\"Let Me In\\" I didn't include because that song deserves its own paragraph.<br /><br />This album was released in late September of 1994. Just a few months before, many of the youth and many of the creative minds of the time were devastated to learn that Nirvana frontman Kurt Cobain, the adoptive father of the Gen-X zeitgeist, had killed himself. Not long before, Michael Stipe had spent time with Kurt and his widow Courtney Love of the bourgeoning riot-grrl band Hole, and they had all become good friends, but there was something that Michael saw in Kurt, which was a deeply-felt pain. After all, both Michael and Kurt had, in many ways, the de facto poster boys of this movement of \\"Alternative Rock\\" that killed the hair-metal pop/rock of old and they were both forced into a much more public existence. While Michael had much more time to prepare for this, and in some ways, celebrated it, Kurt was not cut from the same cloth. The song \\"Let Me In\\" is Michael's cry to the heavens almost of an apology that he lacked the ability to help, or at most, to stop Kurt. The savage, mournful guitar (played not by Peter but by Mike) plays a violent dirge as Michael's raw vocals cut to the very heart of the matter. The agony is clear throughout the song, but nearer tothe end, Peter comes in on one-finger organ playing (that he likened to a roller-rink organ sound in an older interview) and it lifts the song further still. The lack of percussion has been parsed down to Bill simply playing a rhythmic tambourine. It's also worth noting that while I don't know if this song was recorded with Mike playing a guitar belonging to Kurt (given to the band by Courtney), every time the song was played in concert, Mike played it on that guitar. This song of fury and sorrow is, to me, one of the most powerful songs R.E.M ever did and it is the highlight of the album for me.<br /><br />The complaints about the constant sonic assault from the feedback-fuzz sound of Peter's guitar to Mike's heavier bass to Bill's pounding drums is so \\"old\\". It makes the haters of this album seem like they're in their 80's telling those noisy kids down the street to keep it down. Even worse are the complaints that \\"This isn't the old R.E.M!\\". That drives me insane. It's basically the same cries of Beatles fans when they started making albums like REVOLVER. It's not what you grew up with, and that's the whole point. No band can be creatively successful putting out the same old thing album after album. Bands need to evolve. R.E.M needed to, but more importantly, WANTED to evolve, and it wasn't for something as base as \\"cashing in\\". Believe me, they made more than enough money from the sales of their previous two albums to retire very comfortably. I think it's just a cop-out from fans who just wanted Michael, Mike, Peter and Bill to go on forever with the whole college rock/jangly guitar/sweet melodies and harmonies/politically-aware content. While I love that stuff, I also found it in me to love this album and consider it one of their best. I still think that Michael put it best when he called OUT OF TIME their \\"Love\\" album, AUTOMATIC FOR THE PEOPLE their \\"Loss\\" album, and MONSTER their \\"D**k\\" album.<br /><br />When I'm 80, I'm still going to be turning up my hearing aid for MONSTER so that I can show people how rock and roll used to rock in the 90's.the end, Peter comes in on one-finger organ playing (that he likened to a roller-rink organ sound in an older interview) and it lifts the song further still. This song of fury and sorrow is, to me, one of the most powerful songs R.E.M ever did and it is the highlight of the album for me.<br /><br />The complaints about the constant sonic assault from the feedback-fuzz sound of Peter's guitar to Mike's heavier bass to Bill's pounding drums is so \\"old\\". It makes the haters of this album seem like they're in their 80's telling those noisy kids down the street to keep it down. Even worse are the complaints that \\"This isn't the old R.E.M!\\". That drives me insane. It's basically the same cries of Beatles fans when they started making albums like REVOLVER. It's not what you grew up with, and that's the whole point. No band can be creatively successful putting out the same old thing album after album. Bands need to evolve. R.E.M needed to, but more importantly, WANTED to evolve, and it wasn't for something as base as \\"cashing in\\". B0079K4WEE|5.0|PERFECT SENSE begins as a pretensious ARTHOUSE film, with an overwhelming ambition to prove to its viewers just how sensitive, insightful and conceptual another apocalyptic contagious endemic can be. After the pretense of the arty, poetic narration, and the cinematography that offers a veneer of intellectual and emotional depth at every turn, one begins to lose themselves inside the love story, and the symbolic nature of the plot devices. After that tipping point is reached, the film grows on you quickly, as it infects the viewer with its symbolic deconstruction of the human condistion. In the beginning, you have two existencially remote people, Ewan McGregor- a gourmet chief, and Eva Green- a Biologist specialising in Epidemiology, both going thru life, afraid to connect to others too deeply, for fear of their own human deficits. Then, an unknown, bizarre epidemic grips the world. You experience great sadness, and then cant smell. You experience great fear, and cant taste, you experience great hatred, and cant hear, etc. This epidemic is transmitted thru emotional contagions, not germs or viruses. As the gourmet chief loses the basis of his art (taste and smell), and as the epidemiologist loses her ability to save humanity with her own art(biology), the two find they love each other.  Less an apocalyptic thriller true to the genre type, and more a post-modern morality tale discriping how humanity breaks down emotional barriers to one another, PERFECT SENSE lets the viewer consider the ONE perfect sense that humanity will have to depend upon, if life is to go on...TOUCH. As the two lovers touch, at the end of the film, you realize that within the symbolism of the film, they have found the only PERFECT SENSE all humans have---the sense of LOVE, of TOUCH, of knowing someone is CLOSE TO YOU.<br /><br />If you are one to collect european art films, or enjoyed movies like ETERNAL SUNSHINE OF THE SPOTLESS MIND, you're sure to love this film. With the film's saturation of opulent cinematography, sensuous soundtrack, and poetic script, its easy to uncover a new level of meaning, every time you watch it. More so than almost any Scifi genre, this movie demands a suspension of disbelief, and a tacit acceptence of the poetic artifice behind the plot. What is the nature of the apocalypse? Hatred, rage, and fear as our senses shut down in the presence of death? Of course, no doubt. Or, in the final moments of humanity, is meaning to be found in facing the darkness of death, while bravely touching each other's soul with love, with joy, and forgiveness? THis is the great choice facing the film's characters, as it also faces all humanity. This embodies the very HEART, the actual PLOT of the movie. PERFECT SENSE treads new ground, not just in style but also in substance, something few films ever aspire to, let alone achieve.opulent cinematography, sensuous soundtrack, and poetic script, its easy to uncover a new level of meaning, every time you watch it. B005LAIH3G|4.0|\\"John Carter\\" was so brutally savaged by most critics that I felt I was taking a chance buying the four-disk set (which I got at a very good price). Well, it's a delightful, highly entertaining film.<br /><br />Unlike the dreadful \\"Flash Gordon\\", \\"John Carter\\" respects its source. The tone is serious, without arch or campy humor. The story is treated as romantic fantasy, which is what it is. (Michael Giacchino's excellent score sets the right mood.) Though there's a fair amount of action, it's not excessive or gratuitous. The focus is principally on the characters. The ending is highly satisfying, and //almost// draws a tear. Almost.<br /><br />Taylor Kitsch (an unfortunate name for the star of a fantasy film!) isn't a great actor, but he's more than competent. His height (IMDB says he's 6', but he looks a lot shorter) and wiry build make him a believable 19th-century soldier.<br /><br />One assumes Roger Ebert's readers are more-perceptive than the average movie-goer, so it's notable that the viewer rating on Ebert's site is 3 out of 4 stars.<br /><br />Once people who didn't see \\"John Carter\\" in the theater see it on home video, they'll talk it up. \\"John Carter\\" is a good film that needs no apology, and will eventually be considered a classic fantasy.<br /><br />-------------------------------------------<br /><br />ADDENDUM (\\"PS\\" seemed weak)... It's rare to see a film receive such wildly varying audience reviews: \\"A masterpiece!\\" is followed by \\"It's garbage!\\". We all think we have better taste/judgment than those who disagree with us. Being no different, I feel obliged to stick in another two cents.<br /><br />No doubt fanboys (of which I an /not/ one) like films such as \\"John Carter\\" simply because they're acceptable adaptations of a favorite story. Then there are those who detest such films because they don't like the genre, or they find fault with (what they perceive as) \\"bad\\" acting/writing/direction.<br /><br />My all-time favorite film is the magnificently acted and directed \\"Amadeus\\". (Peter Shaffer's screenplay is merely \\"excellent\\". The acting and direction raise it to a perceived level of superbativeness.) I just got the DVD of \\"The Apostle\\", Robert Duvall's tour-de-force of writing, acting, and directing, to replace my LV copy. I'll be taking a course in screenwriting at TheFilmSchool in July (yes, really), and wanted to watch it at least once again. (If you haven't seen this film, /do so/.) I think I know the difference between \\"good\\" and \\"bad\\", writing-, acting-, and directing-wise.<br /><br />What, exactly, were the \\"John Carter\\" haters expecting? It's taken from pulp novels written by a man who felt he could churn out fiction at least as rotten (his word) as the cheap stuff he was reading. The film spends 1/7 its running time giving John Carter a backstory, so we know why he acts as he does. The story plays out as drama (though a weak one); the characters' personalities and interactions are generally plausible. (I don't know how much Michael Chabon contributed, but it's hard to believe he wouldn't have tried to push what is basically an action/adventure tale in the direction of a character-driven story.)<br /><br />\\"John Carter\\" avoids some of the clich&eacute;s of romantic fiction. Carter might be a great sword-swinger, but he won't fight other people's battles. (This is why some viewers incorrectly perceive him as a \\"pacifist\\".) And as Carter still pines for his wife, do you expect him to immediately start drooling over Ms Thoris's thorax? (Though the humanoid Martians are mammalian, the script wisely avoids revealing that they lay eggs. Deejah Thoris is kin to the duck-billed platypus.)<br /><br />I'll agree with the naysayers in one respect... Over the past 30 years, American films have become increasingly cool and  affectless, losing the intense emotions of Hollywood's Golden Age. (Had Michael Curtiz directed \\"John Carter\\", there would have been fewer objections.) It's also true that effects-heavy films can make it hard for directors to keep the focus on the characters, rather than which shots have to be completed that day. I don't like this, but what's a viewer supposed to do? Reject such films altogether?<br /><br />Is \\"John Carter\\" a great (in the absolute sense) film? Of course not. Is it a bad film? Emphatically no. Could it have been better? Definitely. It is what it is -- a well-made adaptation of cheap fiction. If you don't like the latter, fine -- but don't blame the film for being an honest representation of its source.make it hard for directors to keep the focus on the characters, rather than which shots have to be completed that day. If you don't like the latter, fine -- but don't blame the film for being an honest representation of its source.

B007U1FEJE|4.0|I know that some of my fellow Beach Boys fans are so happy that the band is back together that they would give the new record 5 stars even if it were just recordings of Brian Wilson belching. So I'm trying to offset that with a review that doesn't reflect only my own enthusiasm for their reunion. But the great news is: this is a very good album! There are a couple of pleasant-sounding but inconsequential tracks on it, for sure, and the one song that Brian didn't write (Daybreak Over the Ocean) reminds us all why we never bought any Mike Love solo records (love Mike, by the way, but he needs Brian). But there are some truly transcendent songs on this album that sort of cover all previous Beach Boy types. First, as with their earlier records, there are a couple of wonderful pop songs: Spring Vacation, Shelter, and the title song, despite its corny lyrics. And then there are beautiful Brian compositions more similar to the more melodic elements of Smile: Think About the Days to open the album (the final chord change is gorgeous), and the three-song suite at the end of the album. Al has a fantastic lead on From There to Back Again, and the three songs together seem to tell the emotional story of the Beach Boys at this point in their life: some nostalgia, some sadness, and some acknowledgement that death is approaching. Listen to anything released by any other band this year: no one will write a series of songs as complex and beautiful as these three.

Liesel Meminger is quickly introduced as the book thief as she travels to her new foster family with her mother and younger brother.  Her brother dies on the train and Liesel steals her first book, The Grave Digger's Handbook, when it is dropped in the cemetery.  She keeps this a secret until her foster father discovers the book one night.  Together the two explode the words and Liesel's world expands.<br /><br />It is so hard for me to put words to how I feel about this book.  I know that it has been said by so many but this is truly an unique and mind-blowing book, the history and feeling that went into the story writing is more than I have seen in a very long time.  I tend to struggle with books that are set in such a dark time and have many negative issues but this is the exception.  This book made me sit back and really think about this time in our world's history and what it would have been like.<br /><br />I did face one large challenge when it came to reading The Book Thief and that was the style in which it was written.  While I enjoyed the narration from Death it often interrupted the flow of the story for me and I had a hard time getting back on track.  Even though this was a challenge it does not affect my overall feelings and rating of this book.  I do not know any other books that fall into this category for me.  Typically, if I have a hard time with the style I do not enjoy the book as much.  Not so with The Book Thief.

B002S9DRA6|5.0|Enigma is an electronic music group founded in the 1970s, by Michael Cretu. Its vocal & instrumental music is best described as World Beat or New Age Downtempo, with an eclectic touch. It uses Gregorian chants and sexual innuendos, set to a dance beat, whilst vocalising philosophical themes like life after death. The cover designs of their albums are invariably evocative of Gregorian friars, in the garb of monks, their faces hidden below the monk's hood - hence the emergence of the mysterious Enigma that is the name of this music group. The track that shot Enigma to fame is 'Sadeness Part 1.'<br /><br />The Platinum Collection is a 3 cd- set consisting of Greatest Hits; the Remix Collection and the Lost Ones - the latter being so called because these tracks were 'lost in the mists of time' - they remained unpublished till this album was released in the open market. This collection is superbly intriguing and enigmatic - in fact, it nicely rounds off all the tracks of Enigma's music career. The tone of each cd is rich, smooth and mellow - it is positively fantastic when played loud and equally soothing when played soft....this collection is the Ultimate that you can possess - it is legendary in excellence and a true masterpiece in its own right! Just go for it!!!

0062197258|4.0|Catherine is a party girl who enjoys the single lifestyle. When she meets Lee, she is immediately attracted to him and they strike up a relationship. Lee seems wonderful and her friends are all envious of their relationship. But gradually, Catherine starts to find the relationship claustrophobic. Lee starts questioning her about her movements, spying on her, trying to control her. We know from the book's prologue that things are going to go very wrong, but it's not clear exactly how it's going to happen.<br /><br />Meanwhile in a parallel storyline, we are also following a vastly different Catherine some four years down the track - one who is crippled by anxiety and OCD, finds it impossible to open up to anyone and who has clearly been through a horrific experience. A new man has come into her life, but can she trust him? And is she safe from Lee?<br /><br />What's great about this book is the rising tension throughout. There are some small twists and surprises along the way, but overall it's evident that we are building towards some kind of climax - it's just not sure what that will be. The suspense grew so strong that I literally couldn't put it down for the final 100 pages. Up until that point though, I did feel that it was a little too drawn out. Having said that, I liked the way the gradual shifts in Lee and Catherine's relationship - and her gradual recovery in the later story - were portrayed.<br /><br />If you enjoyed this, I strongly recommend [[ASIN:1408809133 The Bed I Made]], another (equally tense) book about a relationship gone sour.
